    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_228524368" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228524368" />
    <a title="Janice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/229024-janice"><img alt="Janice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368587278p2/229024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228524368?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janice" href="/user/show/229024-janice">Janice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/229024-janice?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer228524368&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228524368" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer22100392535644880">I recently read an article about Haruki Murakami and his writing process wherein he divided writers into two distinct categories; you’re either an architect or a gardener. The obvious distinction being that an architect plans out every aspect of their story in advance, whereas a gardener starts out with an idea, and waits to see where it goes. (I know the definitions are obvious in this context, but bear with me, I’ve been conditioned to write this way for three years, and I feel naked without a</span>
  <span id="freeText22100392535644880" style="display:none">I recently read an article about Haruki Murakami and his writing process wherein he divided writers into two distinct categories; you’re either an architect or a gardener. The obvious distinction being that an architect plans out every aspect of their story in advance, whereas a gardener starts out with an idea, and waits to see where it goes. (I know the definitions are obvious in this context, but bear with me, I’ve been conditioned to write this way for three years, and I feel naked without attaching a clear definition or explanation for things.) Murakami himself, identifies himself as a mix of the two. Personally, my experience with writing is limited to an academic or legal setting. I’m not a creative writer, and eschew spontaneity in pretty much every aspect of my life. Thus, I firmly classify myself as an “architect,” (I always wanted to pretend to be an architect!*) I can’t write anything without constructing an outline first - I feel lost without a definite plan. <br /><br />I think Carpenter is an architect that got a little bit sloppy in <i>Hard Rain Falling</i>. I think he started out with a distinct idea of where he wanted the story to go from the outset, and executed it well for the first 2/3 of the novel. In the last 1/3 or so, he started to lose me; and here’s why: he starts out with a protagonist that is completely abominable in every way; despite his tragic beginning, it was truly difficult to find anything redeeming about him, and thus be able to sympathize with him. But, as the story progressed, I suddenly found myself on Jack’s side. I realized that Carpenter had managed to successfully lure me in by slowly revealing the vulnerabilities of an otherwise repugnant character. <br /><br />However, I felt that Carpenter was less successful in developing other aspects of Jack’s evolution. Throughout the story, Jack is a character that is full of rage, and completely devoid of any kind of introspective qualities. Therefore, I thought Carpenter was asking a lot of his readers by accepting certain developments in Jack’s character in the last 1/3 of the book, namely his newly self-aware, philosophical tendencies, his interests in literature, and his feelings on raising children. It didn’t feel natural or consistent with the story to have Jack acquire these characteristics, especially after being released from prison, where he witnessed the violent death of the only person that ever loved him. To me, it’s counterintuitive and inconsistent with his character that he didn’t become more destructive and enraged after that. It seemed problematic that Billy’s death served as a catalyst for Jack’s rehabilitation without more insight on his internal development subsequent to that traumatic event. It’s almost as if my copy of the book is missing 20 pages or so. <br /><br />For the above mentioned reasons, I vacillated between giving this three or four stars. In the absence of an articulable standard of what exactly differentiates a three star book from a four star book, I’ve given this four stars. And admittedly, I’m grading on a curve. It would be unconscionable to give this three stars when I’ve given far lesser books three stars. However, I reserve the right to later amend this rating.<br /><br /><br />*<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0DUPfQCvHXw">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0DUPfQ...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="22100392535644880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2650906564" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2650906564" />
    <a title="David M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43332343-david-m"><img alt="David M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570428545p2/43332343.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2650906564?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David M" href="/user/show/43332343-david-m">David M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/44176428-atjg">ATJG</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2650906564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1766195311078390553">Have to give this one five stars, if for no other reason than because I became quite emotionally attached to the main character.<br/><br/><i>But all I've ever learned from love<br/>was how to shoot at somebody who outdrew you</i><br/><br/>Love as macho game of chicken may, paradoxically enough, still be love. Jack learns to love and be loved despite great odds, and then love teaches him that this is not enough to save him. <br/><br/>If he were still alive today - and I don't think it's likely a guy like that would live this long, but st</span>
  <span id="freeText1766195311078390553" style="display:none">Have to give this one five stars, if for no other reason than because I became quite emotionally attached to the main character.<br /><br /><i>But all I've ever learned from love<br />was how to shoot at somebody who outdrew you</i><br /><br />Love as macho game of chicken may, paradoxically enough, still be love. Jack learns to love and be loved despite great odds, and then love teaches him that this is not enough to save him. <br /><br />If he were still alive today - and I don't think it's likely a guy like that would live this long, but still it's possible - he would be in his nineties; would he still live in San Francisco? In a nursing home maybe. Riddled with dementia and despair, looking back hopelessly on a wasted life? Or maybe, it's just possible, composing Proustian memoirs about what it all means. <br /><br />*<br /><blockquote>But none of that made any difference. You didn't go to jail for what you did; you went because they caught hold of you and didn't know what else to do, and so they put you in jail. They. Yes, they. The filing cabinets in the orphanage. The city hall. The parking meter. The hotel-room door. Batman. Never anybody real sending you to jail. The cops didn't do it. The District Attorney didn't do it. His <i>chair</i> is doing it. Sending my meat and bones to jail, and I got to go along. That's all. Nothing personal. - pp 127 </blockquote><br /><br />It is so fucking frustrating to realize, over and over again, that the enemy is not any embodied person but a social relation. You can assault your boss, but then you'll still have to work. So in the end one might as well just attack a random person on the street.<br /><br />*<br /><blockquote>It struck him with horrible force. His parents, whoever they were, had probably made love out of just such an itch. For fun, for this momentary satisfaction, they had conceived him, and because he was obviously inconvenient, dumped him in the orphanage; because he, the life they had created while they were being careless and thoughtless, was not part of the fun of it all; he was just a harmful side effect of the scratching of the itch; he was the snot in the handkerchief after the nose had been blown, just something disgusting to be gotten rid of in secret and forgotten... A penis squirts and I am doomed to a life of death. - pp 183-84 </blockquote><br /><br />Highly reminiscent of a passage from Querelle. Jack is far more modest in how he adapts, or fails to adapt, to the accidents of life than one of Genet's heroes; Hard Rain Falling is not (and never attempts to be) a work of romantic genius, but rather a realistic novel. Even so, in following Jack's awakening to the ways of the world, a kind of humble and realistic poetry emerges.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1766195311078390553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3304388254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3304388254" />
    <a title="Fioo!" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65819541-fioo"><img alt="Fioo!" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588550645p2/65819541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3304388254?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fioo!" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fioo!" href="/user/show/65819541-fioo">Fioo!</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65819541-fioo?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65819541-fioo?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65819541-fioo?shelf=estados-unidos-washington">estados-unidos-washington</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3304388254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5133608159466033658">"Maybe in the cells they would learn the truth as he had, and know that nothing existed but a single spark of energy, and that spark could die for no reason, and existed for no reason. Then they would understand that it does no good to cry out, because a spark of energy has no ears; the ears are a lie, a joke, a dream, to keep the spark going, and there is no reason to keep the spark going. And more than there is a reason for letting it go out."<br/>—Hard Rain Falling by Don Carpenter.<br/><br/>Oh man...what a</span>
  <span id="freeText5133608159466033658" style="display:none">"Maybe in the cells they would learn the truth as he had, and know that nothing existed but a single spark of energy, and that spark could die for no reason, and existed for no reason. Then they would understand that it does no good to cry out, because a spark of energy has no ears; the ears are a lie, a joke, a dream, to keep the spark going, and there is no reason to keep the spark going. And more than there is a reason for letting it go out."<br />—Hard Rain Falling by Don Carpenter.<br /><br />Oh man...what a book we have here.<br /><br />Published in 1966, Hard Rain Falling could be described as a coming-of-age existentialist prison novel, that starts off in smokey Portland poolhalls, and then roars down the freeway to San Francisco in the 1950s. Rough, wild, deeply tragic and sad. It's a story about lost lonely young men who are dealt a bad hand by life, but oh, it's about so much more.<br /><br />Jack Levitt, our main character, is a tough kid, a true fighter, an orphan. He's done a lot of time for a kid in his 20s, —some of it in horrific states of deprivation and solitary confinement—and it makes him feel, as the book puts it, like "murdering the universe".<br />Billy Lancing is a young black kid from Seattle, a poolhall prodigy. And *spoilers alert* they fall in love.<br /><br />¿Is there any other American author, of that era, that have the courage to portray a relationship between two men like this one? It's UNREAL. This is a beautiful bold book.<br />An american masterpiece, far too ahead of its time. A book that is so all-encompassing, really difficult to summarize. There's a conviction in Carpenter's writing that you immediately believe. True wisdom. It manage to blend so many things into one cohesive narrative that delivers a horrifly, ambiguous and painful blow of an ending.<br /><br />To me, is a real shame that this book didn't become more popular, 'cause its really creates a sense of empathy with those of another race, those of a different sexual orientation, and those who were unlucky enough to have been raised in awful circumstances and found themselves in prison at a young age. And that's a hell of an accomplishment.<br /><br />It's an extraordinarily yet painful, beautiful reminder that life is at the same time magnificent and atrociously cruel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5133608159466033658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_102317826" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102317826" />
    <a title="Ken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730754-ken"><img alt="Ken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565570004p2/730754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/102317826?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/730754-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2010">finished-in-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102317826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12928396716979985150">Where has THIS oddity been hiding since its inception in the 60's? NYRB is certainly to be commended for doing all the hard work and finding gold chips in the salsa for us. Not that I found it Nirvana. Just nervy for its day. It's a pool (billiards, I mean) book, a prison book, and an echoes-of-Hemingway book all in one. Let's start with the hustling.<br/><br/>Carpenter has done some time in a pool hall or two. With the character of Billy Lancing, he captures the thrill of the kill (as in, killing suckers</span>
  <span id="freeText12928396716979985150" style="display:none">Where has THIS oddity been hiding since its inception in the 60's? NYRB is certainly to be commended for doing all the hard work and finding gold chips in the salsa for us. Not that I found it Nirvana. Just nervy for its day. It's a pool (billiards, I mean) book, a prison book, and an echoes-of-Hemingway book all in one. Let's start with the hustling.<br /><br />Carpenter has done some time in a pool hall or two. With the character of Billy Lancing, he captures the thrill of the kill (as in, killing suckers and separating them from their money) as well as the edgy sass and mind games that go into a hustler's talk. This being the 60s and Billy being black (albeit a light-skinned one), race is in the hall, too. Billy utilizes his "handicap" to perfection, using it to gall and taunt his white challengers. End result of these color games? Green. For Billy.<br /><br />But the main character is Jack Levitt, an orphan from practically the get-go. A 20-something tough guy without a job, he's to fistfights what Billy is to pool -- only fistfights pay considerably less. He also likes booze and women (until he hits prison -- more on that later). This book HAD to do time not just in prison but on Bukowski's night stand. Meaning? If riding shotgun with the lowlifes is your thrill, HARD RAIN FALLING might be your book.<br /><br />Eventually Jack lands in San Quentin. Oh, he's in a few other prisons beforehand and it looks like Carpenter did time in the Big House, too -- or so the precise description of Jack's time in the complete darkness of solitary would indicate. It's uncannily real, or what passes for real, given that few readers can compare notes. Billy winds up in prison, too, where he becomes Jack's cellmate. Here Carpenter takes on homosexuality about as frankly as a 60s writer could be expected to. It's three choices, according to him: celibacy, secret masturbation in a place with little privacy, or another guy. The way it's described here, Billy and Jack take a long time to come to it, but they're both in their 20s and feeling the heat, so Door Number 3 it is!<br /><br />Carpenter describes it like so: "It was an arrangement, coldly conceived for sexual satisfaction, without even words that first time, but limited by coldly precise and rational language from there on out. The terms were that they would use each other's bodies for that ornate form of masturbation called Making Love, but there was to be no question of emotional involvement, or prying into one another's soul. This, they decided coldly, would keep them from going crazy or queer." I loved that bit: "an ornate form of masturbation." I don't think the word "ornate" has ever carried such an onerous weight! And note how "cold" that <s>love</s> sex is! "Coldly" has three cameos in one paragraph!<br /><br />After they get out of prison comes the part of the book I like best. On parole, Jack gets a job in a bakery and one day a bunch of rich drunks straight out of Fitzgerald come in, running into this ex-con straight out of Hemingway (well, if Hem's homophobia were unleashed). Jack falls for Sally, a rich girl with a penchant for sex and money (now THERE'S a familiar combo). They try to make a go of it, and the mismatch of this educated spoiled girl and this trying-to-learn ne'er do well (she has him reading ULYSSES, for chrissakes!) is reminiscent of some of the Hemingway mismatched couples we've read in the past. (Note: Jack finds Joyce to be horse crap and prefers FROM HERE TO ETERNITY. Jim Jones fans, take a rare victory!). Anyway, they wed. No kidding. And you get a front row scene to the train wreck. Choo-choo interrupted, and all that! <br /><br />Interesting writing. Not consistent, but certainly good more often than not. This is for the minimalists among us. If you like fancy Henry James and Wee Willie Faulkner sentences longer than a boa before it constricts her, take a pass. Otherwise, get your rain gear and give HARD RAIN FALLING a rip. It may just be the anti-chick lit pill you've been looking for.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12928396716979985150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_100886887" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100886887" />
    <a title="James Thane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3483907-james-thane"><img alt="James Thane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442513566p2/3483907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100886887?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Thane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Thane" href="/user/show/3483907-james-thane">James Thane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100886887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14596825886011051426">This book was initially published in 1966, but was resurrected by George Pelecanos and published anew in 2009 by New York Review Books as part of its Classics series. In his introduction, Pelecanos suggests that it "might be the most unheralded important American novel of the 1960s."<br/><br/>I'm not sure I'd go quite that far, but it is a very good book with brilliantly drawn characters. The main protagonist is Jack Levitt, an orphan whom we first meet on the streets of Portland, Oregon, in 1947. Jack is</span>
  <span id="freeText14596825886011051426" style="display:none">This book was initially published in 1966, but was resurrected by George Pelecanos and published anew in 2009 by New York Review Books as part of its Classics series. In his introduction, Pelecanos suggests that it "might be the most unheralded important American novel of the 1960s."<br /><br />I'm not sure I'd go quite that far, but it is a very good book with brilliantly drawn characters. The main protagonist is Jack Levitt, an orphan whom we first meet on the streets of Portland, Oregon, in 1947. Jack is 17 at the time and has fallen in with a gang of rough boys who live by their wits on the margins of society. <br /><br />The book follows Jack's life until 1960, and it's not a pretty picture. Jack is in and out of trouble and, as a consequence, is also in and out of jail through most of this time. Some of the prison scenes are harrowing but, for that matter, so are many of the scenes when Jack is out on the streets, technically a "free" man.<br /><br />Early on, Jack meets Billy Lancing, a young black pool hustler from Seattle. Jack, who has no special skills or talents, envies Billy who has a great natural gift as a pool player. Initially, Jack targets Billy as a mark, but the two form a bond that will carry them as they cross paths through the years. <br /><br />Jack and Billy both spend a great deal of time contemplating their own lives and the nature of life in general, and there are times when Carpenter extends these ruminations for a bit longer than he should. But that is really the only flaw in this book whose principal strength lies in its characters, even the most minor of whom are fully realized and unforgettable. <br /><br />There is not a single phony or contrived moment in "Hard Rain Falling," and from start to finish, the reader is immersed into a universe that seems completely real and that is totally compelling. This is not a pretty world, but once Carpenter has grabbed hold of you, you can't turn away from watching it. <br /><br />This is very much a book of the early 1960s, and the universe that Carpenter has recreated here is long since gone. He serves up a slice of American life that few of us would want to experience first hand. But readers will be grateful to George Pelecanos and New York Review Books for giving them the opportunity to visit it from a safe distance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14596825886011051426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14348495912351359697" style="display:none">If you’ve exhausted John Williams’ small body of work, this is for you. Christ. The timbre makes the MANY blows antiseptic, and all the better for it. <br /><br />This is how real lives are led. Invisibly. Puffs of smoke that take human form briefly and dissolve. It’s why you see people appearing to hug themselves—it’s an optical illusion. You just can’t see their ghosts. Nor can I. Nor they ours. And by my age, I am choking on smoke. </span>

<div id="review_310931297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/310931297" />
    <a title="Laura Leaney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3633494-laura-leaney"><img alt="Laura Leaney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592839426p2/3633494.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/310931297?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura Leaney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura Leaney" href="/user/show/3633494-laura-leaney">Laura Leaney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer310931297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2117930718969564087">I found this a deeply affecting novel. The book blurb says it's a "Dostoyevskian tale of crime, punishment, and the pursuit of an ever-elusive redemption," but Jack Levitt, the protagonist, is far from feeling the psychological guilt of Raskolnikov in <i>Crime and Punishment</i>. Abandoned in an orphanage in Portland, Oregon, he pursues a life of crime and ends up naked in an isolation cell for four months and three days without light (in a scene so eerie that I doubt I'll ever forget it). There is no </span>
  <span id="freeText2117930718969564087" style="display:none">I found this a deeply affecting novel. The book blurb says it's a "Dostoyevskian tale of crime, punishment, and the pursuit of an ever-elusive redemption," but Jack Levitt, the protagonist, is far from feeling the psychological guilt of Raskolnikov in <i>Crime and Punishment</i>. Abandoned in an orphanage in Portland, Oregon, he pursues a life of crime and ends up naked in an isolation cell for four months and three days without light (in a scene so eerie that I doubt I'll ever forget it). There is no sentimentality in this book. Jack's amorality is purely human, completely understandable, and has nothing to do with God, the devil, or the possibility of the afterlife. <br /><br />As the novel progresses, Jack's awakening - intellectual and emotional - is produced so realistically that there is nowhere I can point to say "this, or this, or this, does not seem believable." Don Carpenter's writing is astounding. The third-person narrative, which is so close to Jack, expresses his heart's deepest knowledge without ever feeling false or over-intellectualized. At the beginning of the novel, Jack thinks: "If he got caught, he accepted his punishment. He had always known that what he had wanted most was freedom, escape from the orphanage, and when he was ready, he escaped. That was all. Then he no longer wanted his freedom, because he had it." By the end of the novel there is this: "Later, after it was all over [. . .] he would realize that the freedom he had always yearned for and never understood was beyond his or any man's reach, and that all men must yearn for it equally." This is not doing justice to his thoughts about freedom, but the depth of his final understanding is inspired. Early in his life, there was not much to love about Jack Levitt, but by the final scene I feel nothing but hope and compassion for him.<br /><br />His time in San Quentin is brilliantly done. The loneliness, the dangerous social systems, race, work details, prison sex - it's all here, written without contrivance. The wardens and guards are not evil. The prisoners are not caricatures. The sex isn't funny or gross. But the social commentary is tremendously interesting. As here: "The perfect convict, the man who lived entirely by the rules set down for him, was not a man but a vegetable. And the constant troublemaker, no matter how sick he was inside, was actually doing just what the State expected of him, therefore justifying the existence of the prison. So it was a matter of delicate balance between defiance and obedience."<br /><br />Dostoyevsky's <i>Crime and Punishment</i> focuses most intently on "punishment," but Don Carpenter's book shows, in all its nuance, HOW redemption might be achieved. It's not through God's salvation. Jack Levitt earns it through his very human efforts, and I am damn proud of him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2117930718969564087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/193056000" />
    <a title="Rayroy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622891-rayroy"><img alt="Rayroy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420266788p2/4622891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193056000?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rayroy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rayroy" href="/user/show/4622891-rayroy">Rayroy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193056000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer547093826137005502"> <br/> Never before have I read a novel that was so true, so raw and so heartbreaking, I was often filled with dread and anxiety for the characters in Hard Rain Falling, because they are flawed and can’t escape who and what they are no matter how hopeful they are, they keep failing in life and it’s as if their doomed from the start. All of this is pretty depressing but I feel Don Carpenter was writing about as realistic as possible, the American Dream simply doesn’t include everyone, and some are far</span>
  <span id="freeText547093826137005502" style="display:none"> <br /> Never before have I read a novel that was so true, so raw and so heartbreaking, I was often filled with dread and anxiety for the characters in Hard Rain Falling, because they are flawed and can’t escape who and what they are no matter how hopeful they are, they keep failing in life and it’s as if their doomed from the start. All of this is pretty depressing but I feel Don Carpenter was writing about as realistic as possible, the American Dream simply doesn’t include everyone, and some are far worse off then others. Right now I’m feeling gloomy after finishing Hard Rain Falling, it just got to me in so many ways.<br /> This book is a must read for anyone who has ever played pool in a basement bar, for the ones who didn’t realize how much they love someone till it was too late, viewers who have tuned into prison documentaries on basic cable, people who try and try and just can’t get ahead, people with ambition but without the tools or the privilege to be ambitious and hell for anyone who enjoys a truly great American literary classic.<br /></span>

  <span id="freeText14631618894603202558" style="display:none">The sad and very poignant story of Jack Levitt, who starts his life in an orphanage and struggles his whole life to rise above the hand that fate has dealt him. <br /><br />It's not a pretty story contained within these pages. <div id="review_1294403807" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1294403807" />
    <a title="Tim Edison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39105523-tim-edison"><img alt="Tim Edison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598386640p2/39105523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1294403807?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim Edison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim Edison" href="/user/show/39105523-tim-edison">Tim Edison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39105523-tim-edison?shelf=20th-century-american-literature">20th-century-american-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1294403807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2834931718634381250"><br/><br/>Don Carpenter does not appear to be an author revelling in fame. Actually he is not revelling in anything as he died about twenty years ago. However if the quality of his novel Hard Rain Falling is an accurate compass, he should probably be considered one of the great 20th century American authors. <br/><br/>Hard Rain Falling is simply brilliant. Written completely without ostentation or pretence, it is a rigorous study of the suffering and isolation of those who are born without the benefit of good fortu</span>
  <span id="freeText2834931718634381250" style="display:none"><br /><br />Don Carpenter does not appear to be an author revelling in fame. Actually he is not revelling in anything as he died about twenty years ago. However if the quality of his novel Hard Rain Falling is an accurate compass, he should probably be considered one of the great 20th century American authors. <br /><br />Hard Rain Falling is simply brilliant. Written completely without ostentation or pretence, it is a rigorous study of the suffering and isolation of those who are born without the benefit of good fortune. For what else can explain those who are born into wealth and glory and those who are not. <br /><br />Well......it is more than this, too. It is also the narration of a man who drags himself from the abyss of hard luck and degradation to find a modicum of redemption and mercy. Through an orphanage, a reform school and finally prison, Don Carpenter examines how an individual attains a kind of freedom from the more oppressive components of the societal machine without submitting himself to isolation from the society.<br /><br />In the great tradition of Hubert Selby and Charles Bukowski, Don Carpenter has made a stunning and absorbing contribution to 20th century American realism. <br /><br />Every Night &amp; every Morn <br />Some to Misery are Born <br />Every Morn and every Night <br />Some are Born to sweet delight <br />Some are Born to sweet delight <br />Some are Born to Endless Night <br /><br />From William Blake's Auguries of Innocence</span>
  <a data-text-id="2834931718634381250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82571142?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/698826-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/698826-alan?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82571142" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6202497921247904230">Xmas present from my youngest daughter - boy she really listens - I only mentioned this in passing after reading a review in the Guardian, something like 'that sounds good' and a couple of weeks later here it is...<br/>I've abandoned 'Day' for it, and read the first 50 pages today waiting to get in the bathroom (it's my eldest's 20th and we're all going out). Hard and fast stuff. Excellent. <br/><br/>Now read about 90 pages and it just gets better and better, it is almost miraculously good..<br/><br/>SPOILER ALERT...<br/><br/>Thi</span>
  <span id="freeText6202497921247904230" style="display:none">Xmas present from my youngest daughter - boy she really listens - I only mentioned this in passing after reading a review in the Guardian, something like 'that sounds good' and a couple of weeks later here it is...<br />I've abandoned 'Day' for it, and read the first 50 pages today waiting to get in the bathroom (it's my eldest's 20th and we're all going out). Hard and fast stuff. Excellent. <br /><br />Now read about 90 pages and it just gets better and better, it is almost miraculously good..<br /><br />SPOILER ALERT...<br /><br />This is how I experienced this book: read up to page 60 thinking yes this is good stuff, appreciating it, the set up, the characters 'hardboiled' and petty criminals, a lot about pool about which I know little, then Billy a black newcomer to the scene is invited to a party and waits for a lift, after standing at a <i>big smelly urinal</i> trying to pee, near to tears and thinking he is unwanted and left, and then the car arrives. For some reason from this moment, I was absolutely there, something about the unflashy prose had worked its way into me, it was more than just a good book, it was great, I was getting in the crowded car with them, Billy sitting on Jack Levitt's lap - <i>the only place he could sit - he said, 'now every time we go over a bump, you owe me a dollar'.</i> I was at the party, held in a place they break into and wreck, where Jack falls asleep drunk. He is awakened by two big plainsclothesmen, fights them, gets coshed, ends up in reform school where his urge to kill (<i>not the result of a process of brutalization</i>) lands him in solitary confinement. Here <i>after a while time ceased to exist. Time slipped in a strange way. First he was aware there was no present, no such thing as a moment.. At first he could not see because there was no light in his cell; then that became a delusion, and he could not see because he could not see, and then even that lost its reality, he could not see because there was nothing to see and never had been - his mind had tricked him into believing there were colors and shapes and he knew there were no such things..<br /><br />..But then there would be a clank and a rattle at the door, followed by the smell of food and everything would rush back into him as the smell of food hit him and aroused him and he would begin to tremble and slather and giggle.</i><br /> <br />The book goes on to follow Jack and Billy's lives and they meet again in prison, in San Quentin where they have a sexual relationship. There are some moving moments here, one especially 'Brokeback Mountain' moment later but I don't want to spoil it too much for prospective readers. (I do wonder if Annie Proulx read this though before she wrote BM).<br /><br />Jack goes on to marry a rich girl (or so he thinks), and the book broadens out to include meditations on marriage and fatherhood. The book is wide ranging, and will frequently break off to consider questions of existence and race and relationships which sounds pretentious, and not normally something I like, but it grows naturally out of the story.<br /><br />It is hard to quote a passage that will show you why this book is so good, because it is the cumulative effect of the story and the simple prose, the clear eyed view of the author which makes it so, it might take you a little while to feel it but by page 60 you will be away. <br /><br />I read it over new year and had to interrupt it frequently for family commitments etc. If I could I would have sat in a corner and read it straight from cover to cover. What a great start to 2010.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6202497921247904230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_105848863" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105848863" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105848863?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105848863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9761461698711891087">A tough novel to categorize, Hard Rain Falling isn’t going to do it for you if you need a book that offers warm fuzzies and a happy, feel-good ending. It is dark, gritty and real, a no-holds barred kind of novel that goes well beyond the much overdone “angry young man” trope to become a story that is intrepidly honest. Considering its initial publication date of 1966, it’s also a novel much ahead of its time in the way that the author deals with racism, homosexuality and the harshness of unreaso</span>
  <span id="freeText9761461698711891087" style="display:none">A tough novel to categorize, Hard Rain Falling isn’t going to do it for you if you need a book that offers warm fuzzies and a happy, feel-good ending. It is dark, gritty and real, a no-holds barred kind of novel that goes well beyond the much overdone “angry young man” trope to become a story that is intrepidly honest. Considering its initial publication date of 1966, it’s also a novel much ahead of its time in the way that the author deals with racism, homosexuality and the harshness of unreasoned authority.<br /><br />The main character is Jack Levitt, whose parents were young and stupid when he was born, leaving him in a hellhole of an orphanage. Jack runs away from there when he is older, lands in Portland, Oregon. <br /><br /><br />There he meets Billy Lancing, a young black man from Seattle who gets by as a pool hustler, and with whom Jack finds friendship. But Jack can't stay out of trouble, and eventually lands in a reform school, where he spent much of his time in a dark cell in solitary confinement after nearly killing one of the guards. There <br /><br /> "At times, all his senses deserted him, and he could not feel the coldness of the concrete or smell his excrement, and the small sounds he made and the sounds that filtered in through the door gradually dimmed, and he was left alone inside his mind, without a past to envision, since his inner vision was gone, too, and without a future to dream, because there was nothing but this emptiness and himself. It was not uncomfortable, not comfortable. These things this did not exist. It was colorless, senseless, mindless, and he sometimes just disappeared into it." (81)<br /><br /><br />This feeling of utter isolation pervades Jack Levitt's character throughout the novel. Eventually Jack finds himself in San Quentin on a trumped-up charge. It is there he meets up again with Billy Lancing, who maneuvers things so that he and Levitt become cell mates, and what starts out as just prison sex turns into something else, a human and feeling connection between the two, although Jack can't bring himself to admit it until it's too late and Jack is once more in the depths of loneliness. And even though Jack is eventually somewhat transformed after his release, when he marries and has a kid, the freedom he envisioned in the past continues to escape him.<br /><br />Hard Rain Falling is a book that is raw in emotion. Every character is real and feels. This in itself is an incredible achievement - I can't think of another book in which the characters are so powerfully alive, especially Jack. And while so many novelists are into the game of blaming society for an individual's lifelong ills, that's really not the case here. As George Pelecanos notes in the introduction to this story, "the damage done to Jack at his very core can never truly be healed (xi)," and Jack notes that underneath it all, it wasn't really society that had abandoned him, but his parents.<br /><br />Truly an amazing novel that I can recommend wholeheartedly. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9761461698711891087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3096946280" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3096946280" />
    <a title="AB" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75799600-ab"><img alt="AB" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578033264p2/75799600.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3096946280?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="AB" class="user" itemprop="url" name="AB" href="/user/show/75799600-ab">AB</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3096946280" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5804061628740612896"><i>"Who else?" Jack wanted to know. "Who else went down the tubes?"<br/>"Well," Mano grinned. "You." </i><br/><br/><br/><i> Hard Rain Falling </i> is an exercise in pessimism and nihilism. Jack Levitt is given a tough break and it keeps him down and out for the entire book. The book is tough and so is Jack. Probably about as 'hard boiled' as it gets. Out of this genre, that I suppose can be labeled as being in the realm of 'noir', it was pretty good. There were many moments throughout the book that could be described as the pure</span>
  <span id="freeText5804061628740612896" style="display:none"><i>"Who else?" Jack wanted to know. "Who else went down the tubes?"<br />"Well," Mano grinned. "You." </i><br /><br /><br /><i> Hard Rain Falling </i> is an exercise in pessimism and nihilism. Jack Levitt is given a tough break and it keeps him down and out for the entire book. The book is tough and so is Jack. Probably about as 'hard boiled' as it gets. Out of this genre, that I suppose can be labeled as being in the realm of 'noir', it was pretty good. There were many moments throughout the book that could be described as the pure essence of noir. The most obvious being <i> When you lose, you lose forever, an when you win, it only lasts a second or two. That's life. <br /></i>. Not to mention the tough outcast character who has a chip on his shoulder.<br /><br /><br />I really liked this book, I stop short at saying I loved it because I felt some moments of part 3 were a bit overdone. But the great moments really outshine any gripes I may have. The first two parts were unbelievably good. The vivid descriptions of Jack spending four months in solitary confinement were well done. But the star for me was the discussions of homosexuality in prison. It wasn't pulpy like how a lot of shock sex was done in these type of books. It was erotic, coldly logical, and touching. Then came part 3. It was still enjoyable but some of Jack's introspective moments became a bit long winded. I get what was going on, but coming out of the first 2 parts, the change of gear was a bit off putting. That being said, it was still enjoyable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5804061628740612896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1109756619" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1109756619" />
    <a title="Daniel Villines" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines"><img alt="Daniel Villines" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536179787p2/2266055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1109756619?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Villines" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Villines" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines">Daniel Villines</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1109756619" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2265921690855239105">I once spent five hours flying on board an aircraft watching consecutive episodes of <i>Lockup Raw</i>. I was hooked by the inmates and their stories. In episode after episode, prisoners, outcasts of society, were interviewed and each one told of their crimes (or of their innocence) in an honest straight forward manner. While on camera, they acted like they were talking to their next door neighbor. And yet, during all those hours of <i>Lockup Raw</i>, there was never an understanding of who these people were </span>
  <span id="freeText2265921690855239105" style="display:none">I once spent five hours flying on board an aircraft watching consecutive episodes of <i>Lockup Raw</i>. I was hooked by the inmates and their stories. In episode after episode, prisoners, outcasts of society, were interviewed and each one told of their crimes (or of their innocence) in an honest straight forward manner. While on camera, they acted like they were talking to their next door neighbor. And yet, during all those hours of <i>Lockup Raw</i>, there was never an understanding of who these people were and the question of choice was never resolved. Why couldn't these criminals simply decide to not do the things they did?<br /><br /><i>Hard Rain Falling</i> provides some portion of understanding into who these people might be through its main character, Jack Levitt. While Jack Levitt was an outcast of society, it was not due to his poor decisions. Jack made the best decisions possible based on the content of his beliefs. It was also not due to his laziness. Jack worked as hard as he could to the physical and intellectual limits of his abilities. Nor was it due to a disinterest in learning. Jack had a hunger to learn and constantly sought to expand his understanding of the world. And it was not due to some sort of pure evilness of heart. Jack pursued love to the point of pain. If Jack Levitt was an outcast of society, then the only real reason for his position in life was the randomness associated with his creation.<br /><br />The strength of this book rests in the intricate development of its characters in the first two parts of the book. Carpenter succeeds in defining characters that are rational unto themselves, while at the same time appearing irrational when viewed from a societal perspective. Reasons for violence and crime abounded in the mind of Jack Levitt even though society would see things differently. This duplicity of perspective was engrossing and it makes <i>Hard Rain Falling</i> into a reading experience.<br /><br />The book's shortcomings rest in the third and final part. After being drawn into Carpenters world of complex characters, the third part of the book seems light and rushed. Rather than achieving a sense of duplicity that was previously relished, the characters in the third part of the book seemed, at times, contradictory. <i>Hard Rain Falling</i> is still a great book, but another hundred pages devoted to the last part of the book would have made it unique.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2265921690855239105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yngMC+3/St3/VzlkmSX4F2OUWL4ajxQX3jXyFrt6eA06hWx966MJ98OGareMlN/Rbf2YOQQNftm6SSufvoH42A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

It tells the story of two young boxers out of Stockton, California: Ernie Munger and Billy Tully, one in his late teens, the other just turning thirty, whose seemingly parallel lives intersect for a time. Set in an ambiance of glittering dreams and drab realities, it tells of the two fighters\' struggles to escape the confinements of their existence, and of the men and women in their world. Fat City is a novel about the sporting life like no other ever written: without melodrama or false heroics, written with a truthfulness that is at once painful and beautiful.\n\nDenis Johnson: &quot;Between the ages of 19 and 25 I studied Leonard Gardner’s book so closely that I began to fear I’d never be able to write anything but imitations of it, so I swore it off(...)When I was about 34 (the same age Gardner was when he published his), my first novel came out. About a year later I borrowed Fat City from the library and read it. I could see immediately that ten years’ exile hadn’t saved me from the influence of its perfection — I’d taught myself to write in Gardner’s style, though not as well. And now, many years later, it’s still true: Leonard Gardner has something to say in every word I write.&quot;\n\nJoan Didion: &quot;Leonard Gardner\'s Fat City affected me more than any new fiction I have read in a long while, and I do not think it affected me only because I come from Fat City, or somewhere near it. He has got it exactly right--the hanging around gas stations, the field dust, the relentless oppressiveness of the weather, the bleak liaisons sealed on the levees and Greyhound buses--but he has done more than just get it down, he has made it a metaphor for the joyless in heart.&quot;\n\nDavid Wagoner: &quot;The people he writes about are alive and three-dimensional, and have that meaty, sweaty immediacy I admire in novels and find so seldom. It\'s an odd, interesting world he explores here--as tense and vivid as the prose.&quot;\n\nIvan Gold: &quot;Gardner writes with power, with an insider\'s knowledge, and with a vividness and love for his characters which redeem them even when they\'re lost and beaten.&quot;\n\nHarry Mark Petrakis: &quot;A man of real talent. He makes the savage world he writes of come alive to the point where the reader can smell the sweat, and feel the anguish of unremitting failure.&quot;\n\nRoss Macdonald: &quot;In his pity and art Gardner moves beyond race, beyond guilt and punishment, as Twain and Melville did, into a tragic forgiveness. I have seldom read a novel as beautiful and individual as this one.&quot;\n\n\nOriginally published in 1969, Fat City is an American classic whose stature has increased over the years. First published in the 1950s, at the height of the McCarthy era, <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17281116817075993343\" style=\"display:none\">Oakley Hall\'s legendary Warlock revisits and reworks the traditional conventions of the Western to present a raw, funny, hypnotic, ultimately devastating picture of American unreality. First published in the 1950s, at the height of the McCarthy era, Warlock is not only one of the most original and entertaining of modern American novels but a lasting contribution to American fiction.\n\n&quot;Tombstone, Arizona, during the 1880\'s is, in ways, our national Camelot: a never-never land where American virtues are embodied in the Earps, and the opposite evils in the Clanton gang; where the confrontation at the OK Corral takes on some of the dry purity of the Arthurian joust. Oakley Hall, in his very fine novel Warlock has restored to the myth of Tombstone its full, mortal, blooded humanity. Wyatt Earp is transmogrified into a gunfighter named Blaisdell who . . . is summoned to the embattled town of Warlock by a committee of nervous citizens expressly to be a hero, but finds that he cannot, at last, live up to his image; that there is a flaw not only in him, but also, we feel, in the entire set of assumptions that have allowed the image to exist. . . . Before the agonized epic of Warlock is over with--the rebellion of the proto-Wobblies working in the mines, the struggling for political control of the area, the gunfighting, mob violence, the personal crises of those in power--the collective awareness that is Warlock must face its own inescapable Horror: that what is called society, with its law and order, is as frail, as precarious, as flesh and can be snuffed out and assimilated back into the desert as easily as a corpse can. It is the deep sensitivity to abysses that makes Warlock one of our best American novels. For we are a nation that can, many of us, toss with all aplomb our candy wrapper into the Grand Canyon itself, snap a color shot and drive away; and we need voices like Oakley Hall\'s to remind us how He washes up in Butcher’s Crossing, a small Kansas town on the outskirts of nowhere. Butcher’s Crossing is full of restless men looking for ways to make money and ways to waste it. Before long Andrews strikes up a friendship with one of them, a man who regales Andrews with tales of immense herds of buffalo, ready for the taking, hidden away in a beautiful valley deep in the Colorado Rockies. He convinces Andrews to join in an expedition to track the animals down. The journey out is grueling, but at the end is a place of paradisal richness. Once there, however, the three men abandon themselves to an orgy of slaughter, so caught up in killing buffalo that they lose all sense of time. Winter soon overtakes them: they are snowed in. Jack Black\'s autobiography was a bestseller and went through five printings in the late 1920\'s. It has led a mostly subterranean existence since then - best known as William S. Burrough\'s favorite book, one he admitted lifting big chunks of from memory for his first novel, Junky. But it\'s time we got wise to this book, which is in itself a remarkably wise book - and a ripping true saga. It\'s an amazing journey into the hobo underworld: freight hopping around the still wide open West at the turn of the 20th century, becoming a member of the &quot;yegg&quot; (criminal) brotherhood and a highwayman, learning the outlaw philosophy from Foot-and-a-half George and the Sanctimonious Kid, getting hooked on opium, passing through hobo jungles, hop joints and penitentiaries. This is a chunk of the American story entirely left out of the history books - it\'s a lot richer and stranger than the official version. This new edition also includes an Afterword that tells some of what became of Black after he wore out the outlaw life and washed up in San Francisco, wrote this book and reinvented himself.<\/span>\n Now, twenty-two years later, the two men lie in adjacent beds in a VA hospital.Over the course of a day and a night, Walter James and Braiden Chaney talk of memories, of passions, of fate. With great vision, humor, and courage, Brown writes mostly about love in a story about the waste of war.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5423182083703296306\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));;       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/261458.Don_Carpenter">About Don Carpenter</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4709022500054259530">Don Carpenter was an American writer, best known as the author of Hard Rain Falling. He wrote numerous novels, novellas, short stories and screenplays over the course of a 22-year career that took him from a childhood in Berkeley and the Pacific Northwest to the corridors of power and ego in Hollywood. A close observer of human frailty, his writing depicted marginal characters like pool sharks, pr</span>
  <span id="freeText4709022500054259530" style="display:none">Don Carpenter was an American writer, best known as the author of Hard Rain Falling. He wrote numerous novels, novellas, short stories and screenplays over the course of a 22-year career that took him from a childhood in Berkeley and the Pacific Northwest to the corridors of power and ego in Hollywood. A close observer of human frailty, his writing depicted marginal characters like pool sharks, prisoners and drug dealers, as well as movie moguls and struggling actors. Although lauded by critics and fellow writers alike, Carpenter's novels and stories never reached a mass audience and he supported himself with extensive work for Hollywood. Facing a mounting series of debilitating illnesses, Don Carpenter committed suicide in 1995.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4709022500054259530" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/454931-hard-rain-falling">Quotes from Hard Rain Falling</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;How do you wake up? It was one thing to know that you had been asleep all your life, but something else to wake up from it, to find out you were really alive and it wasn't anybody's fault but your own. Of course that was the problem.<br /><br />All right. Everything is a dream. Nothing hangs together. You move from one dream to another and there is no reason for the change. Your eyes see things and your ears hear, but nothing has any reason behind it. It would be easier to believe in God. Then you could wake up and yawn and stretch and grin at a world that was put together on a plan of mercy and death, punishment for evil, joy for good, and if the game was crazy at least it had rules. But that didn't make sense. It had never made any sense. The trouble was, now that he was not asleep and not awake, what he saw and heard didn't make sense either.<br /><br />Mishmash, he thought. You know enough to know how you feel is senseless, but you don't know enough to know why.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/847612-how-do-you-wake-up-it-was-one-thing-to">19 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;All night long, in his cell, he burned with hatred. It did not matter what he thought, it was how he felt; and alone in the darkness of his cell, with the muttering noises of the tank around him, he felt like murdering the universe.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
